Ruling of Collective Judgment and Necessity of Agreement
Defending the validity of collective judgment as a single ruling entity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position on collective judgment and apply the principle that a group is treated as a single legal entity.

Core Idea
A group of judges is one judge; agreement is inherent, and authority defines membership, not number.

In this passage, the author defends the Hanafi view that a group appointed to judge functions as one person. This has implications for fatwa: a collective mufti body's ruling is a single ruling, and no explicit consensus is needed. We will analyze the arguments, analogies, and the debate over the minimum number.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Read the opening: the group's individuals are not intended; rather, they stand in the position of the imam or judge. The key claim is that the group is a single entity.
2
Analyze the analogies
The author uses testimony and grammar examples to show that a plural can be treated as singular in legal effect. Note the reference to women's testimony and grammatical rules.
Pay attention to the phrase 'Do you not see...' which introduces these analogies.
3
Examine the condition of agreement
The author argues that agreement among the group is obligatory and cannot be otherwise because they are like one judge. This means their ruling is automatically unified.
The author dismisses the need for a preponderator when they differ, as disagreement is impossible in essence.
4
Evaluate the dispute on minimum number
Some Maliki scholars require three for a jamā'ah linguistically. The Hanafi response is that the group is defined by attribute, not number, so even one can constitute the group.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: practical effect on validity of a single member's ruling.
5
Assess the practical consequence
The passage concludes that if a group delegates to one, that one is still from the group by attribute. Appointing one initially suffices according to the Hanafi view.
This directly impacts fatwa councils: a single mufti from a council can issue a valid fatwa.

Secondly, regarding the first aspect: i.e., when the group is appointed... (the response): May Allah guide us and you to the straight path. Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal argument defending the ruling that a group of people, when collectively appointed to judge, function as a single judge. The author addresses several objections: (1) The group's ruling is valid even if only one of them acts, because they are considered a single entity in legal effect, like a judge. (2) The requirement of agreement among the group is inherent because they are like one person; their consensus is automatically implied. (3) The analogy to the case of two litigants is sound, but the Hanafi and Maliki schools have clear texts on this. (4) Regarding the delegation of authority to one person from the group, the author finds no explicit text but argues that if they delegate, that delegate is considered part of the group by attribute. (5) The minimum number for a group is debated: some argue three, others that even one suffices by virtue of the group's attribute. The author sides with the view that a group is defined by attribute, not number, so even one person can constitute the group if he has the requisite attribute.

Key Terms
جماعة (jamā‘ah): A group of people appointed collectively to perform the function of judging; considered as a single legal entity in this context.
قاضي (qāḍī): A judge appointed by the ruler to adjudicate disputes; his ruling is singular and binding.
اتفاق (ittifāq): Consensus among the members of the group; argued to be automatically present because they are like one person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the minimum number of persons required to constitute a 'جماعة' for the purpose of collective judgment?
A) Three, because the word 'جماعة' linguistically implies at least three.
B) Two, by analogy to testimony of women.
C) One, if that person possesses the attribute of authority.
D) Five, following the Maliki school.

Key Takeaway
A group of judges or muftis is legally equivalent to a single judge; their ruling is one, and agreement is automatic. The group is defined by authority, not number.

Conditions for Judgment by a Group and Minimum Number of a Jama'ah
Clarifying conditions and minimum number for a judicial jama'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a jama'ah is defined by attributes not persons, and evaluate the minimum number requirement for a judicial panel.

Core Idea
A judicial jama'ah is defined by qualifications, not count; its minimum is three for validity.

This passage from Thanwi addresses nuanced questions about the composition and authority of a judicial group (jama'ah). It clarifies that the group's identity depends on shared attributes, not fixed persons, and that the minimum number for a valid jama'ah is three. The discussion also covers delegation of authority and the separation between a judge's power to decree and enforce rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The passage responds to objections about the conditions for a group's judgment to be binding. Identify the main questions: agreement on opinion, delegation, minimum number, and conditions beyond appointment.
2
State the contested points
List the specific disputed points: (1) Do group judges need unanimous agreement? (2) Can a group delegate to one member? (3) What is the minimum number for a jama'ah? (4) Are additional conditions (like enforcement) required?
3
Extract the key legal maxim
The author states: 'The group is not limited by persons but by attributes.' Understand how this maxim governs delegation and membership.
This is a sughra (minor) maxim in the qawida framework.
4
Analyze the dispute on minimum number
Some scholars argue for a minimum of three, citing linguistic and customary definitions. Others allow a single qualified person. Trace the arguments and the author's reliance on later authorities like al-Adawi, al-Dasuqi, and Hajar.
The author leans toward three as the verified (tahqiq) position.
5
Apply the refined rule to delegation scenarios
Consider two delegation cases: (a) a group delegates after a case is referred, (b) initial delegation to one member. Apply the maxim that the delegate must possess the attributes of the group.
Delegation to an outsider is not addressed; the author finds no explicit text.

Commentary
This passage is a legal discussion (rasm al-mufti) from Thanwi's work dealing with conditions for the validity of judgments rendered by a group of judges (jama'ah) and the minimum number required for a jama'ah. The author first responds to an objection: some argued that for a group's judgment to be binding, they must unanimously agree on a single opinion, drawing an analogy to two independent judges who must agree. The author counters that this matter is established by primary texts, not analogy, though the analogy is sound in his view. He then addresses the delegation of authority from a group to one of its members, stating that they have not found an explicit text permitting it, but if the group delegates to an individual, that individual must be from among them since the group is defined by attributes (qualifications) not specific persons. The minimum number of a jama'ah is disputed: the author reports that a group of scholars insist the minimum is three, citing authorities like al-Adawi, al-Dasuqi, and Hajar. The fourth question asks whether certain conditions are required for the judge's appointment. The author clarifies that such conditions are not from the essential requirements of judgeship but arise from the supreme imam's authority. He then discusses the scope of a judge's power—a judge creates rulings, but enforcement is a separate function that may or may not be delegated. Finally, the author uses the topic to support the permissibility of appointing a judge even under non-Muslim rule if Muslims need it, citing al-Dasuqi and al-Sawi who consider the appointment of a judge a Muslim ritual (sha'irah). The passage concludes with a reference to a fatwa by al-Maziri.

Key Terms
جماعة (jama'ah): A group of qualified judges or persons with authority to decide legal matters
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation of authority from a group to a single person to render judgment
ولاية القضاء (wilayat al-qada'): The authority of a judge to create (insha') rulings, which may be separate from the power of enforcement

Rasm al-Mufti: The Judge's Authority and Enforcement Power
Ruling on scope of judge's authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between a judge's authority to issue rulings (wilāyat al-qaḍāʾ) and the power of enforcement (al-siyāsa al-ʿāmma), and apply this distinction in fatwa practice.

Core Idea
A judge's authority to judge does not include enforcement; enforcement is a separate delegation.

This lesson examines a foundational rasm al-mufti rule: the scope of a judge's authority. The passage clarifies that the power to issue binding judgments is inherent in the office of judge, but the power to enforce those judgments is an additional authority that must be explicitly granted. This distinction is crucial for muftis when determining the validity of a judge's ruling, especially when the judge lacks enforcement capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question being addressed
The passage begins by asking whether certain conditions are required for a judge's appointment. Note the phrase 'هل يشترط'. Identify what condition is being discussed.
The condition in question is the power of enforcement, not the basic validity of the judge's appointment.
2
Parse the answer and the distinction
The answer states that enforcement is not a condition for the validity of the judge (shart sihhat al-qadi). Instead, it is an additional matter (amr za'id) arising from the ruler. Distinguish between the core authority to judge and the executive power.
The key phrase: 'ليست من شروط صحة القاضي بل هي أمر زائد عليه'.
3
Define the key terms
Identify the two key terms: 'wilāyat al-qaḍāʾ' (judicial authority) and 'al-siyāsa al-ʿāmma' (general political power/enforcement). Use the legal definition dissector to understand their components.
These terms are distinct in Hanafi legal theory.
4
Understand the authority hierarchy
The passage explains that the judge's role is limited to creating obligations (insha' al-ilzam). Enforcement (quwwat al-tanfidh) is a separate delegation from the ruler. Recognize that even a weak judge who cannot enforce rulings still has valid judicial authority.
The example of a judge weak in power over tyrant kings illustrates this.
5
Apply to fatwa practice
Consider how a mufti would apply this rule. If a judge issues a ruling but lacks enforcement capacity, the ruling is still valid. The mufti should not advise disregarding the ruling simply because it cannot be enforced.
This is a practical application of the rasm al-mufti rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع هل يشترط [إلى قوله: لا تحصل بمجرد نصبهم] تجوابه: إنها ليست من شروط صحة القاضي للمذكورة في أبواب القضاء بل هي أمر زائد عليه ينشأ من الإمام الأعظم لأن القضاء وما يستفاد به من النظر في الأحكام وما ليس للقاضي النظر فيه إلى أن قال: فأما ولاية القضاء فقال القراقي: هذه الولاية [متنادلة] للحكم لو يندرج فيها غيره. وقال أيضا في موضع: وليس للقاضي السياسة العامة لاسيما الحاكم الذي لا [يده] له على التنفيذ كالحاكم الضعيف القدرة على الملوك الجبابرة فهو ينشيء الإلزام على الملك العظيم ولا يخطر له تنفيذه [التعذر] ذلك عليه بل الحاكم من حيث هو حاكم ليس له إلا الإنشاء وأما قوة التنفيذ [عامر] زائد على كونه حكما [تقاد] يفوض إليه التقيد وقد لا يندرج في ولايته. انتهى مرادنا منها اختصارا.
Fourth: Is it required [to his statement: 'It is not obtained by merely appointing them']? The answer: They are not from the conditions of the validity of the judge mentioned in the chapters of judiciary, but rather they are an additional matter arising from the Greatest Imam, because the judiciary and what is used in examining rulings and what the judge does not have authority to examine, until he said: As for the authority of the judiciary, al-Qaraqi said: This authority is [delegated] for judgment, whether other things are included in it or not. And he also said in another place: The judge does not have general political power, especially the ruler who has no hand over execution, like the ruler weak in power over tyrant kings. He creates the obligation upon the great king, and execution does not occur to him, it is difficult for him. Rather, the ruler inasmuch as he is a ruler has only creation (of rulings). As for the power of execution, it is an additional matter beyond being a judge, it is delegated to him, and it may not be included in his authority. End of our purpose from it in abridgment.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the conditions for a judge's appointment. It clarifies that certain conditions, like being appointed by a Muslim ruler, are not prerequisites for the validity of a judge's rulings in all situations. The text quotes al-Qaraqi to distinguish between the authority to create rulings (institution of legal obligations) and the power to enforce them. The judge, by virtue of his office, has the authority to issue judgments, but enforcement is a separate power that must be explicitly delegated. This is particularly relevant when the judge is appointed by a non-Muslim authority, as the ruling focuses on the inherent authority of the judiciary.

Key Terms
ولاية القضاء (wilāyat al-qaḍāʾ): The authority of the judiciary to issue binding judgments
السياسة العامة (al-siyāsa al-ʿāmma): General political governance and enforcement

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Neither issuing nor enforcing rulings

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fatwa practice, a judge's ruling is valid based on his judicial authority alone; enforcement power is separate and not a condition of validity.

Presumption of 'Adalah in Dar al-Harb Testimony
Parsing the rule on acceptance of testimony from a Dar al-Harb judge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on accepting testimony from a judge in Dar al-Harb and identify when his 'adalah may be questioned.

Core Idea
A judge's testimony is accepted based on his apparent uprightness; mere residence in Dar al-Harb does not automatically invalidate it.

This lesson examines a fatwa scenario where a woman seeks to prove her husband's death through testimony from a judge living in Dar al-Harb. The objection arises whether the judge's residence under non-Muslim rule compromises his legal integrity. The Hanafi response clarifies the principle of basing rulings on apparent conditions rather than speculative assumptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Understand the fatwa context: a woman claims her husband died in Dar al-Harb, and testimony from a local judge is presented. The question is whether that testimony is acceptable given the judge's residence under non-Muslim rule.
The judge's role: he is both a witness and an adjudicator.
2
Recognize the Twofold Objection
The objector raises two points: (1) the judge's own 'adalah is questionable because staying in Dar al-Harb may be prohibited; (2) his appointment by non-Muslims may invalidate his jurisdiction. This lesson focuses on the first aspect.
The second aspect is covered in the next lesson.
3
Apply the Principle of Apparent Uprightness
The author states that we base rulings on the apparent state of the person (zahir al-'adalah). We do not reject testimony based on mere possibilities or weak conjectures. The default assumption is that the person is upright unless clear evidence shows otherwise.
This principle is grounded in the obligation to think well of Muslims (husn al-zann).
4
Distinguish between Compulsion and Voluntary Choice
If the judge's stay in Dar al-Harb was due to necessity (idṭirār) or for a valid religious purpose (e.g., to call to Islam), his 'adalah is not affected. If he stayed voluntarily for worldly reasons (e.g., trade without a valid interpretation), that becomes a defect in 'adalah.
The difference depends on evidence; if unclear, the principle of excuse applies.
5
Examine the intra-madhhab Difference
Some scholars reject the testimony of one who voluntarily enters Dar al-Harb for trade, but the Hanafi position is not to reject based on a single possibility unless there is clear proof that his stay was voluntary.
The mufta bihi is the lenient view: acceptance unless strong counter-evidence.

بحكم الجاهلية والاعراض عن التأويل اختيارا فهذا قدح في عدالته واختلف امل المذهب في رد شهادة الداخل اختيار التجارة ضمن ظهرت عبد الله منهم وشك في اقامته على اى وجہ فالاصل عذرك لان محل الاحتمالات السابقة شهد لعذر لا فلا يرد دلاحتمال واحد الوان توجد قوان تشهد ان اقامته كانت اختيار الالوجه واما الوجه الثاني وهو تولية الكافر للقضاة والامناء وغيرهم لج حز الناس بعضهم عن بعض فقدادی بعض اهل المذهب أنه واجب عقلاوان كان با طلا قولیة الكافر لهذا القاضي اما لطلب الرعية او اقامته لهم للضرورة لذلك فلا يطرح حكمه وينفذ كما لو ولاه سلطان مسلماه وفي البيضاوى عند قول رب العزة قال اجعلني على خزائن الارض الى حفيظ عليه قيه دليل على جوان طلب التولية واظهارات مستعد لها والتولي من يد الكافي إذا علمانه لاسبيل إلى إقامته سياسته الحلق الا بالاشتهار به اهو في تبصرة الحكام فصل قال المازني في شرح التلقين القضاء ينعقد باحدث جهين احدهما عقدا مير المؤمنين او واحد من امرائه الذين جعل الهم العقد في مثل هذا و الثاني ذو الراى واهل العلم والمعرفة والعدالة الرحيل منها كملت فيه شروط القضاء وهذا حيث لا يمكنهم مطالعة الإمام في ذلك ولاان يستدعوا منه ولايته ويكون عندهم له نیا به من حقد الإمام الاعظم او نيابة عمن جعل له الامام ذلك للضرورة الداعية إلى ذلك . وأما قولكم الخامس فان كانت القوة والشوكة وإلى قوله بينوا بالدليل فجوات ما اللعنا على نسوههم في كتب المذهب التي بايدينا ان القوة والشركة من شروط الجماعة واما الفراق بين القاضي والجماعة فيين وذلك ان القاضى وعمله جزء من اجزاء عمل وظيفة الامام وعدم الامام شرط الوجود At her judge or just witnesses: Is that accepted from them or not, while it is a necessity and it is not known whether her stay there under the people of disbelief is compulsion or choice? He answered, may Allah have mercy on him, the objection in this has two aspects: The first includes the judge and his apparent deficiency in integrity because staying in Dar al-Harb is not permissible under the leadership of the people of disbelief. The second is from the aspect of jurisdiction, since the judge is appointed by the people of disbelief. The first is a reliable basis in this issue and similar ones, and it is to think well of Muslims and keep the sinner away from them, so it should not be deviated from due to imaginary possibilities and weak conjectures, like permitting one who appears upright (even though he may commit in hatred and in reality have committed a major sin unless proof of his infallibility is established – this allowance is discarded, and the ruling is based on the apparent, which is the most lenient, unless something appears in the situation that necessitates departure from uprightness, then one must pause until the remaining cause of the departure from uprightness is removed, and the ruling remains based on the dominant opinion thereafter. Then he said: This resident in Dar al-Harb if he is compelled, then there is no problem that he is not advanced in [question of] his integrity? And if his interpretation is correct, like his stay in the land of war hoping to attract the people of war and convert them from falsehood, as indicated by al-Baqillani and as indicated by the followers of Malik in permitting entry for the sake of a captive. But if he stayed according to the laws of ignorance and turning away from [proper] interpretation voluntarily, then this is a defect in his integrity. And the people of the school differed regarding rejecting the testimony of one who enters [dar al-harb] voluntarily for trade, within [the context of] what appeared from Abdullah and doubt about his stay on any basis – the principle is [that he is excused] because the locus of previous possibilities indicates excuse, so it is not rejected due to a single possibility unless there exist rules that testify that his stay was voluntary. As for the second aspect: it is the appointment by a non-Muslim of judges and trustees and others to protect people from one another. Some of the people of the school considered it rationally obligatory, even if [the appointment] is outwardly invalid. The non-Muslim's appointment of this judge, either because of the subjects' request or his establishment for them out of necessity, therefore his judgment is not dismissed and is executed, just as if a Muslim sultan appointed him. And in al-Baydawi, at the saying of the Lord of Might: {He said: Appoint me over the treasures of the earth, indeed I am a knowing guardian} there is evidence for the permissibility of seeking appointment and showing readiness for it. And appointment from the hand of a non-Muslim when one knows that there is no way to establish the policy for the people except by accepting it. So it is. In Tabsirat al-Hukkam, chapter: al-Mazini said in Sharh al-Talqin: Judgeship is established by one of two ways: one is by contract of the Commander of the Faithful or one of his governors to whom he delegated the contract in such matters; the second is by the people of opinion and knowledge and uprightness, those qualified, when they are able to do so, and they appoint someone in whom the conditions of judgeship are complete. And this is when they cannot consult the Imam in that matter nor seek his appointment, and they have someone who takes the place of the Great Imam or in place of one for whom the Imam gave that [authority] due to necessity. As for your fifth statement: if the power and strength [are needed] etc. up to his saying: show with evidence the gaps in what we mentioned about their women in the books of the school that are in our hands – that power and sharing are conditions of the community. As for the separation between the judge and the community, it is clear that the judge and his work are part of the work of the Imam's function, and the absence of the Imam is a condition for the existence of the community since they said: if the ruler is absent, a group of upright people takes his place. As for your sixth statement: if disagreement occurs between two groups... up to his saying: so what is the way?
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage from Thanwi's Al-Hilatul Najijah is a detailed fatwa discussion combining two interrelated issues: (1) the acceptance of testimony from a judge or witnesses in Dar al-Harb regarding a woman's claim of her husband's death, and (2) the validity of a judge's appointment by a non-Muslim ruler. The text responds to objections posed by an interlocutor, systematically addressing each. 

The first issue centers on whether a woman's testimony or that of a judge in Dar al-Harb should be accepted given the potential defect in the judge's 'adalah (integrity). The author identifies two aspects: the judge's own uprightness—since staying in Dar al-Harb under non-Muslim rule may be prohibited—and the validity of his appointment by non-Muslims. Regarding the first, the author argues that we should presume good of Muslims and not hastily reject their testimony based on mere possibilities. As long as the judge's apparent state is one of 'adalah, his testimony is accepted. However, if there is clear evidence that his stay was voluntary and for worldly reasons (like trade without proper ijtihad), then his 'adalah is compromised. The Hanafi madhhab differs on rejecting the testimony of a Muslim who voluntarily enters Dar al-Harb for trade; the preferred view is not to reject based on a single possibility unless clear proof indicates his stay was voluntary.

The second issue explicitly deals with a non-Muslim appointing a Muslim judge. The author cites some Hanafi scholars who consider it rationally obligatory (wajib 'aqlan) to accept such an appointment if it is the only way to administer justice, even if the appointment process is invalid in principle. The judge's rulings are enforceable as if appointed by a Muslim ruler. Evidence is drawn from the Qur'anic story of Yusuf (AS) seeking appointment from a non-Muslim ruler (Pharaoh), and from the principles of necessity (darura). The text also references al-Mazini's classification of how judgeship is established: either by formal appointment from the caliph or his delegate, or by the community of qualified Muslims when there is no access to the caliph—again emphasizing necessity.

The passage concludes with responses to further objections about power (quwwa) and community formation, asserting that the judge and the community are linked to the imam's function, and in his absence, the community may act.

Key Terms
تولية (tawliyah): The act of appointing a person to a position of authority, here specifically a judge or trustee.
عدالة (ʿadālah): Legal uprightness; the quality of being trustworthy and avoiding major sins, which is a condition for the acceptance of testimony and the validity of judgments.
دار الحرب (dār al-ḥarb): The abode of war; a non-Muslim territory where Islamic law is not enforced and where Muslims may be subject to non-Muslim rule.
اضطرار (iḍṭirār): Compulsion or dire necessity that permits otherwise prohibited actions or excuses deficiencies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on the testimony of a judge in Dar al-Harb if his stay was for a valid religious purpose (like da'wah)?
A) Accepted
B) Rejected
C) Requires further investigation
D) Only accepted if appointed by a Muslim ruler

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts testimony from a judge in Dar al-Harb based on his apparent uprightness unless clear evidence of voluntary sinful stay is proven.

Validity of a Judge's Appointment by a Non-Muslim Ruler: Necessity Overrides Form
Parsing the rule on appointment by non-Muslims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a judge appointed by a non-Muslim ruler and identify the role of necessity in overriding the formal requirement of appointment by a Muslim authority.

Core Idea
A judge appointed by a non-Muslim ruler is valid if necessary for the administration of justice; his rulings are enforceable like those of a Muslim-appointed judge.

This lesson tackles the concern that a judge's appointment by a non-Muslim ruler might invalidate his decisions. The Hanafi response invokes necessity (darura) and the community's need for justice. Drawing on Qur'anic precedent and legal principles, the text establishes that such appointments are effective when there is no other way to establish a judicial system.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the Second Objection
The objector claims that the judge's appointment by the people of disbelief (ahl al-kufr) invalidates his jurisdiction. This lesson addresses that issue.
This is separate from the 'adalah issue.
2
Identify the Hanafi Response
Some Hanafi scholars consider it rationally obligatory (wajib 'aqlan) to accept such an appointment when necessary. Even if the appointment process is invalid in principle, the rulings are executed as if appointed by a Muslim sultan.
The key is necessity (darura).
3
Analyze the Evidence from Qur'an and Scholarship
The text cites the story of Prophet Yusuf (AS) who sought appointment from a non-Muslim ruler (Pharaoh), showing that seeking power from a non-Muslim is permissible when done for a valid purpose. Also references al-Mazini's classification of how judgeship is established: either by caliphal appointment or by appointment by the community of qualified Muslims when the caliph is unreachable.
This supports the validity of necessity-based appointments.
4
Apply the Maxim of Necessity
The underlying legal maxim is 'necessity permits prohibitions' (al-darura tubih al-mahzurat). Since establishing justice is obligatory, the means (accepting appointment from non-Muslims) becomes permissible.
The judge's rulings are enforceable.
5
Consider the Community's Role
The passage also discusses that when the imam is absent, the community of upright people can appoint a judge. This reinforces that the source of authority is the community's need for justice, not solely the formal appointment.
This aligns with the principle that the judge's function is part of the imam's role, but others can deputize.

Non-Muslim Appointment of Judges and Finality of Judgments
Ruling on non-Muslim appointments and finality of judgments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a judge appointed by a non-Muslim under necessity has enforceable judgments, and identify when a judgment from a qualified group is final and cannot be overturned.

Core Idea
A judge appointed by a non-Muslim under necessity is valid; conflicting judgments from qualified groups are not overturned.

This lesson addresses two interconnected issues in the Hanafi rules of judiciary: the validity of judicial appointments made by non-Muslim rulers, and the finality of judgments issued by qualified groups (jamā‘at al-‘udūl) when no single Imam is available. The source passage draws on classical Hanafi works and the commentary of al-Baydawi, illustrating how necessity (ḍarūra) can override the general principle that only a Muslim Imam has authority to appoint judges. It also provides guidance on resolving conflicts between different judicial bodies, emphasizing that once a judgment is properly issued, it cannot be overturned by another group.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue of non-Muslim appointment
Recognize that the passage discusses a scenario where a non-Muslim ruler appoints a judge. The general rule is that such an appointment is invalid (bāṭil), but the passage introduces an exception based on necessity.
This exception is a classic example of Hanafi istiḥsān.
2
Understand the necessity principle
The passage states that if the appointment is made at the request of the subjects or out of necessity, the judge's rulings are enforced as if appointed by a Muslim sultan. This is supported by the precedent of Prophet Yusuf seeking office in a non-Muslim administration.
Reference to Tafsir al-Baydawi on Qur'an 12:55.
3
Distinguish between individual judge and collective group
The passage explains that when the Imam is absent, a group of upright Muslims (jamā‘at al-‘udūl) can assume judicial authority. The judge's work is part of the Imam's office, but the absence of the Imam is a condition for the group's existence.
This distinction clarifies the separate roles.
4
Apply the rule of finality of judgments
The passage addresses the sixth objection: if two qualified groups issue conflicting judgments, the original judgment is final. A later judge cannot overturn it unless the first judgment was improperly made (e.g., due to hidden evidence).
This upholds judicial stability (ḥukm qaṭ‘ī).

وهو تولية الكافر للقضاة والأمناء وغيرهم لجذب الناس بعضهم عن بعض. فقد قال بعض أهل المذهب أنه واجب عقلا وإن كان باطلا. تولية الكافر لهذا القاضي إما لطلب الرعية أو إقامته لهم للضرورة لذلك فلا يطرح حكمه وينفذ كما لو ولاه سلطان مسلم. وفي البيضاوي عند قول رب العزة: {قال اجعلني على خزائن الأرض إني حفيظ عليم} فيه دليل على جواز طلب التولية وإظهار الاستعداد لها. والتولي من يد الكافر إذا علم أنه لا سبيل إلى إقامته سياسة الحق إلا بالاشتهار به اهـ. وفي تبصرة الحكام: فصل: قال المازني في شرح التلقين: القضاء ينعقد بأحد وجهين: أحدهما: عقد أمير المؤمنين أو واحد من أمرائه الذين جعل لهم العقد في مثل هذا. والثاني: ذو الرأي وأهل العلم والمعرفة والعدالة الرحيل [؟] منها كملت فيه شروط القضاء، وهذا حيث لا يمكنهم مطالعة الإمام في ذلك ولا أن يستدعوا منه ولايته ويكون عندهم له نية به من قبل الإمام الأعظم أو نيابة عمن جعل له الإمام ذلك للضرورة الداعية إلى ذلك. وأما قولكم الخامس: فإن كانت القوة والشوكة إلى قوله بينوا بالدليل فجواب ما ذكرنا على نسوههم [؟] في كتب المذهب التي بأيدينا أن القوة والشوكة من شروط الجماعة. وأما الفراق بين القاضي والجماعة فيبين [؟] وذلك أن القاضي وعمله جزء من أجزاء عمل وظيفة الإمام وعدم الإمام شرط الوجود الجماعة لأنهم قالوا إذا فقد الحاكم أن جماعة العدول تقوم مقامه. وأما قولكم السادس: لو وقع الاختلاف بين جماعتين إلى قوله فكيف السبيل إلى ترجيح حكم أحدهما على الآخر فجوابه أن استوفت الجماعة شروط الحكم الشرعي بأن تقول بعد أداء الزوجة حجتها وإنكار الزوج لك [؟] شهور [؟] وقالت نعم وأحضرتها ثم قالوا للزوج لك الطعن فيهم وقال لا أعذر [؟] ثم حكموا لها فليس يجوز له أن يرفع هذه النازلة إلى غيرهم ولا لهم نقض هذا الحكم. وفي مختصر الشيخ خليل وشرحه لدردير: رفع حكم الخلاف في تلك النازلة فلا يجوز للمخالف فيها نقضها. فإذا حكم بفسخ عقد أو صحته لكون بد [؟] في ذلك لم يجز لقاض غيره ولا له نقضه ولا يحجر [؟] لصفت [؟] علم بحكم أن يفتي بخلافه. وفي المواق على مختصر الشيخ خليل ونص المدونة: قال مالك: وجه الحكم في القضاء إذا ادعى الخصمان وفهم القاضي عنهما وأراد أن يحكم بينهما أن يقول لها؟؟؟ [؟] وجاء [؟] بقيد لكما حجة فان قالا لا حكم بينهما ثم لا يقبل منه حجة بعد إنفاذ [؟] حكم. ولو قال له بقيت لي حجة أمهله فان لم يأت بشيء حكم عليه فإن أتيا بعد ذلك يريدان نقض ذلك لم يقبل منهما إلا أن يأتيا بأمريرى [؟] سأل محمد [؟] لأن لذلك وجها. قال ابن القاسم: مثل أن يأتي شاهد عند من يقضي بشاهد ويمين ويقول الخصم لا علم لي بشاهد آخر فيحكم عليه القاضي ثم يجد شاهد آخر بعد الحكم فيقضى بهذا الآخر، ومثل أن يأتي ببينة لم يعلم بها وأما أشبه ذلك، ولم يقبل منه؟؟؟ إذا جاء الحكم على وجهه فحكم القاضي الثاني باطل لا [؟] مشاركة بينهما حتى يصور الخلاف بينهما والعكس فالأول باطل كذلك. أعص [؟] أما إذا وقع الخلاف قبل الحكم بأن تنازعا بين القاضيين فالقول للطالب [؟] منهما كما في مختصر الشيخ خليل وشرحه لدردير: واذا...
And that is the appointment of a non-Muslim for judges, trustees, and others to attract people away from each other. Some people of the madhhab said that it is rationally obligatory, even if it is invalid. The appointment of a non-Muslim for this judge is either upon the request of the subjects or his establishment for them out of necessity; therefore his judgment is not overturned and is executed as if a Muslim sultan appointed him. In al-Baydawi, concerning the word of the Lord of Majesty: 'He said: Appoint me over the treasuries of the land; indeed I am a knowledgeable keeper.' Therein is evidence for the permissibility of seeking appointment and showing readiness for it. And accepting appointment from a non-Muslim when one knows that there is no way to establish the policy of truth except through this appointment. End quote. In Tabsirat al-Hukkam: a chapter: al-Mazini said in Sharh al-Talqin: Judgeship is contracted in one of two ways: first, by the contract of the Commander of the Faithful or one of his commanders whom he has given the right of contract in such matters. Second, the person of opinion, knowledge, and uprightness whose qualifications are complete, and this is where they cannot consult the Imam in that nor request his appointment, and they have the intention from the Supreme Imam or from a deputy appointed by the Imam due to necessity. As for your fifth statement: if power and authority... until his words: explain with evidence. The answer to what we mentioned concerning their ... in the books of the madhhab that we have is that power and authority are conditions for the group. As for the distinction between the judge and the group, it is clear; that is because the judge and his work are part of the work of the Imam's office, and the absence of the Imam is a condition for the existence of the group because they said: if the ruler is absent, a group of upright people takes his place. As for your sixth statement: if a dispute occurs between two groups... until his words: how is the way to prefer the judgment of one over the other? The answer is that if the group fulfills the conditions of legal judgment, such as after the wife presents her evidence and the husband denies her witnesses and she says yes and produced them, then they said to the husband: you have the right to challenge them, and he says no excuse, then they rule in her favor, then it is not permissible for him to raise this case to others, nor can they overturn this judgment. In Mukhtasar al-Shaykh Khalil and its commentary by al-Dardir: The judgment of disagreement in that case is raised, so it is not permissible for the opponent to overturn it. If he rules to annul a contract or its validity for a reason in that, it is not permissible for another judge or anyone else to overturn it, nor is it binding for one who knows a ruling to give a fatwa contrary to it. In al-Mawaq on Mukhtasar al-Shaykh Khalil and the text of al-Mudawwana: Malik said: The manner of judgment in judiciary: when the two litigants claim, and the judge understands from them and wants to judge between them, he should say to them... They said: no judgment between them, then no evidence is accepted from them after the execution of the judgment. If one says: I have remaining evidence, he gives him time; if he does not bring anything, he rules against him; if they come afterwards wanting to overturn that, it is not accepted from them unless they bring something compelling because that has a basis. Ibn al-Qasim said: such as when a witness comes before one who judges with one witness and an oath, and the opponent says: I do not know of another witness, then the judge rules against him, then he finds another witness after the judgment, then he judges with this latter one. And such as when he brings evidence that he did not know about, and similar matters, and it is not accepted from him... When the judgment has been issued properly, then the judgment of the second judge is invalid; there is no cooperation between them until the disagreement between them is realized, and vice versa, so the first is also invalid. As for if the dispute occurs before the judgment, such that the two judges argue, then the statement is of the one who claims from them, as in Mukhtasar al-Shaykh Khalil and its commentary by al-Dardir: and if...
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hilatul Najijah discusses two key issues in the rules of judiciary: the validity of a judge appointed by a non-Muslim ruler, and the resolution of conflicting judgments from different groups. The author begins by noting that some Hanafi scholars consider appointment by a non-Muslim rationally obligatory if it prevents greater harm, even though the appointment is technically invalid (bāṭil). However, if the non-Muslim appoints a judge at the request of the subjects or out of necessity, the judge's rulings are enforced as if appointed by a Muslim sultan. This is supported by a reference to Tafsir al-Baydawi on the story of Prophet Yusuf (AS), where seeking public office is considered permissible when one is capable and just. The passage then cites Tabsirat al-Hukkam, stating that judgeship can be contracted in two ways: (1) by the direct appointment of the Imam or his authorized delegate, and (2) by a qualified person taking the role when the Imam cannot be contacted, due to necessity, with the intention of acting as his deputy. The text then addresses objections: the fifth objection concerns the requirement of power and authority (quwwa wa shawka) for the group (jamā‘a) that acts in place of the missing Imam. The response clarifies that power is a condition for the group, not for the individual judge. The sixth objection asks how to resolve conflicting judgments between two groups. The answer is that if the group fulfills all conditions of legal judgment, their ruling is final and cannot be overturned by another group, even if the latter holds a different opinion. The exception is if the first judgment was issued improperly, then a second judge may intervene. This is illustrated with examples from Mukhtasar Khalīl and al-Mudawwana, such as when a litigant later produces a witness or evidence that was previously unknown. The passage underscores the Hanafi principle that judicial finality (ḥukm qaṭ‘ī) is upheld once all procedural conditions are met, and conflicting later rulings are void unless the first judgment was flawed. The OCR is severely corrupted, but the legal reasoning remains discernible.

Key Terms
تولية (tawliya): Appointment to a position, here specifically the act of a ruler appointing a judge.
القضاء (al-qaḍā'): Judgeship; the office of issuing binding judgments in disputes.
جماعة العدول (jamā‘at al-‘udūl): A group of upright, qualified Muslims who may collectively assume judicial authority in the absence of an Imam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a non-Muslim ruler appoints a judge out of necessity and the judge is qualified?
A) The judge's rulings are void and must be rejected.
B) The judge's rulings are enforced as if appointed by a Muslim sultan.
C) The judge must be reappointed by a Muslim authority.
D) The appointment is valid only for family law cases.

Key Takeaway
Necessity validates a non-Muslim's appointment of a judge, and once a qualified group issues a judgment, it cannot be overturned by another group.

When Litigants Disagree on Which Judge to Approach
Sets rules for precedence and procedure when litigants want different judges

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of precedence and lots to determine which judge hears a case when litigants disagree.

Core Idea
The claimant chooses the judge; if both claim, precedence of messenger or lot decides.

When two parties litigate, they may disagree on which judge should hear the case. This lesson explains the Hanafi procedure for resolving such disagreement, prioritizing the claimant but using precedence and lots when both are claimants.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Parties
Determine who is the claimant (مطالب) – the one demanding a right – and who is the defendant (مطلوب). The claimant has the initial right to choose the judge.
If roles are clear, the claimant's choice prevails.
2
Check if Both Are Claimants
If each party demands something from the other (mutual claims), both are considered claimants. The simple rule does not apply.
This is the key nuance in the passage.
3
Precedence by Messenger
Check if one party sent a messenger to the other requesting him to come to a specific judge. The one whose messenger preceded has the right to choose the judge.
The messenger acts as a proof of prior demand.
4
Resort to Lots
If no messenger preceded, or both come together each claiming to be the claimant, lots (قرعة) are drawn to determine which judge will hear the case.
Lots are a last resort when no other criterion distinguishes.
5
Handle Different Claims
If each demands a different thing, each may choose his own judge independently. Priority goes to the one who first approached a judge, or whose messenger came first; otherwise lots again.
This is from al-Dasuqi's gloss.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنازع الخصمان فأراد أحدهما الرفع لقاض وأراد الآخر الرفع لقاض آخر كان القول للمطالب وهو صاحب الحق دون المطلوب. ثم إن العريكن طالب مع مطلوب بأن كان كل يطالب صاحبه رفع إلى من؟ أي سبق رسوله لطلب الإتيان عنده، وإن لم يسبق رسول قاض بل ستوبا في المجيء مع دعوى كل أنه المطالب أقرع للقاضي الذي يذهبان إليه فمن خرج محمد للذهاب له نھبالہ كالادعاراي كما يقدم بينهما في الادعاء بعد ابتانهما للقاضي الذي أقرعا في الذهاب إليه أو الذي اتفقا على الذهاب. ثم تنازعا في تقديم الدعوى إذا الموضوع أن كلا طالب اه. وقال الدسوقى فى حاشية عن هذا الشرح تنبيه قد علم من المصنف الحكم فيما إذا اتحد المدعى به وكان كل من المتداعيين يطالب الآخر به على ما قال الشارح. وأما إذا كان كل منهما يطلب صاحبه بشيء مغاير لما ادعي به الأخر ففي نقل المراق وابن عرفت من المارزى أن كل واحد منهما أن يطلب حقه عند من شاء من القضاة فإذا ادى أحدهما على صاحبه عند قاض وفرغ فلصاحبه أن يدعي عليه عند من شاء فان اختلفا فيمن يتدى الطلب أو فيمن يذهبان إليه أولا من القاضيين فان سبق أحدهما القاض ترجح قوله وان ذهب كل منهما لقاضي فالمعتبر من سبق رسوله من القضاة وإن لم يكن لأحدهما ترجيح بسبق الطلب على الأخر وله بغير ذلك أقرع بينهما. وأما قولكم ان قبل في حل هذه السؤال إلى قوله كما هو مشاهد وجوابه ان نسب الجماعة بأشخاصه لفصل الخصام معدوم نصه عندنا كما تقدم يل هم تعينوا بالأوصاف فمن الصف بهذه الأوصاف فهو منهم وعليه لا باس ان يرفع كل ذي دعوى إلى صالح جيرانه من العدول متعدد الجماعة بقدر الحاجة كما جاز تعدد القضاة مطلقا
The two litigants dispute; one wants to raise (the case) to a judge, and the other wants to raise to another judge. The statement is of the claimant, who is the owner of the right, not the defendant. Then if both are claimant and defendant, like each is demanding from his companion (in a mutual claim), to whom should they raise? It depends on whose messenger preceded in requesting the other to come to his (judge). And if no messenger preceded, but they both come together while each claims he is the claimant, lots are drawn for the judge to whom they go. Whose lot comes out goes to him... as is done between them in the claim after they appear before the judge for whom they drew lots or agreed to go. Then they disputed about the presentation of the claim when the matter is that both are claimants. And al-Dasuqi said in his gloss on this commentary: 'A note: it is known from the author the ruling when the claimed thing is one and each of the two litigants demands the other with it, as the commentator said. But if each demands from his companion something different from what the other claims, then according to the transmission of al-Maraq [?] and Ibn Urfah from al-Marizi [?], each one may demand his right from whichever judge he wishes. So if one sues his companion before a judge and finishes, then his companion may sue him before whomever he wishes. If they differ as to who should raise the demand first or which judge they should go to first, then if one precedes the judge, his statement is preferred. And if each goes to a judge, then consideration is given to whose messenger from the judges preceded. And if neither has preference by precedence of demand or otherwise, lots are drawn between them. As for your statement that before in solving this question... up to his saying 'as is observed', and its answer is that the grouping of people by their persons for settling disputes is nonexistent in our school, as previously mentioned. Rather, they are specified by descriptions. Whoever is characterized by these descriptions is of them. Therefore, there is no harm in every claimant raising (his case) to the righteous among his neighbors of the upright, multiple groups as per need, just as multiplicity of judges is absolutely permissible.'
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage, from a work on legal stratagems, provides a rule (rasm) for muftis and judges when litigants disagree on the choice of judge. It begins with a clear case: one litigant wants to go to one judge, the other to another judge. The default rule is that the claimant (مطالب) – the one who demands his right – has the choice, not the defendant (مطلوب). The text then examines a more complex scenario where both parties are simultaneously claimants and defendants, such as in a mutual claim. In such a case, the deciding factor is which party first sent a messenger to the other requesting him to come to the first party's chosen judge. If neither preceded, or they come together each insisting they are the claimant, lots (قرعة) are drawn to determine which judge to approach. Once the judge is determined by lot or agreement, they present their claims before him. The passage then references a commentary by al-Dasuqi, who distinguishes between cases where the subject of the claim is the same (both demand the same thing) versus cases where each demands something different. In the latter scenario, each litigant has the right to pursue his claim before whichever judge he wishes, independently. If they disagree on whose claim should be heard first, the one who first approaches a judge has priority. If they come simultaneously, the one whose messenger reached the judge first is preferred; otherwise, lots are drawn. The final part addresses an objection about grouping people by persons rather than descriptions, but the ruling affirms that as long as the individuals have the required qualifications (like being righteous and just), they can act as arbiters, just as multiple judges are allowed.

Key Terms
المطالب (al-muṭālib): The claimant, the one who demands his right from the other party.
المطلوب (al-maṭlūb): The defendant, the one from whom a right is demanded.
الاقراع (al-iqrāʿ): Drawing lots to decide between equal claimants when no other criterion exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When two litigants both claim against each other (mutual claims) and disagree on which judge to go to, and neither sent a messenger first, how is the judge chosen?
A) The plaintiff's choice prevails
B) The defendant's choice prevails
C) Lots are drawn for the judge
D) Each goes to his own judge

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when litigants disagree on a judge, the claimant chooses; if both claim, the first to send a messenger decides, otherwise lots are drawn.

Conditions for a Group of Muslims as Judges: Qadi vs Mufti
Answers six objections regarding appointment and conditions of a group of Muslim judges.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relaxed conditions for a group of Muslims acting as judges, distinguish the roles of qadi (executor) and mufti (opinion-giver), and apply the Hanafi principle that a collective body need not meet all conditions of a single qadi.

Core Idea
A group of Muslims can judge with relaxed conditions; the qadi enforces, the mufti advises.

This lesson unpacks a passage from Ashraf Ali Thanwi's 'Al-Hilatul Najijah' that addresses six objections regarding the appointment of a group of Muslims as judges. You will learn that a collective body does not require each member to possess all qualifications of a lone qadi, and you will grasp the critical distinction between a qadi (who holds executive power) and a mufti (who issues non-binding opinions). These principles are essential for understanding Hanafi judicial and fatwa practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection and its answer
The objection claims a group must meet all conditions of a single qadi. The author replies that the group, as a collective, need not have all his attributes or complete all conditions.
This is the core principle: collective deputation relaxes individual requirements.
2
Analyze the second objection about unanimity
The objection requires unanimous agreement among the group. The author compares them to two arbitrators (ḥakamayn), noting that their agreement is only needed for the specific incident, not ongoing; their appointment is not perpetual.
The condition of unanimity is limited to the case at hand.
3
Distinguish qadi from mufti using the fourth objection
The question is whether a qadi must have executive power (quwwat al-tanfīdh). The answer is yes – the qadi is a executor, while the mufti merely gives opinions. This is the foundational difference.
The mufti has no enforcement authority; his fatwa is advisory.
4
Apply the ruling on errors inside vs outside the madhhab
If the group's verdict contradicts a ruling outside the Hanafi madhhab, it is void. If inside the madhhab, it stands only if they followed preponderant opinions and principles; negligence invalidates it.
The first judgment removes disagreement if correct within the madhhab.
5
Understand the principle of general appointment
The author notes that in India, general consensus of Muslims (without explicit ruler appointment) suffices for a qadi, as there is no fear of opposition.
This reflects the context of non-Muslim rulers; the community's agreement legitimizes the judge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

By way of review and clarification, excuse me: sixth issue, which is like the summarized and branching from each other. Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage discusses six questions regarding the appointment of a group of Muslims as judges (qadis) and the conditions for their decisions to be valid. The author, Ashraf Ali Thanwi, is responding to objections that might arise from the earlier discussion. The first question establishes that undertaking all conditions (such as being a mujtahid, male, etc.) is necessary for a single qadi, but for a group of Muslims acting collectively, not all conditions are required. The second question addresses the requirement that a qadi be single, while a group is multiple; the answer explains that a group does not need to have all the attributes of a single qadi. The third question is about delegation to one person; the author says it is not permissible. The fourth question concerns execution power; the qadi must have enforcement authority (quwwat al-tanfidh), which distinguishes him from a mufti, who only gives opinions. The fifth and sixth questions deal with errors: if the group's decision is outside the madhhab, it is invalid; if inside, it stands if they followed preponderant opinions and conditions. The passage also notes that a mufti is not required to enforce his verdicts, while a qadi must. The reference to 'no opponent' indicates that general appointment by Muslims suffices in India, even without explicit opposition to oppressors.

Key Terms
قاضي (qāḍī): A judge appointed to decide disputes and enforce rulings; he has executive power (quwwat al-tanfidh).
مفتى (muftī): A jurist who issues fatwas (non-binding legal opinions) without the power to enforce them.
جماعة المسلمين (jamā‘at al-muslimīn): A group of Muslims appointed collectively to act as judges; they need not meet all conditions of a single qadi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A qadi must be male, a mufti may be female
B) A qadi has executive power to enforce rulings, a mufti only gives opinions
C) A mufti must be a mujtahid, a qadi need not be
D) A qadi is appointed by the ruler, a mufti is self-appointed

Key Takeaway
A group of Muslims can serve as judges with relaxed conditions, and the critical distinction between mufti and qadi is the power of enforcement.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
8 min
The Mufti as Mujtahid, the Qadi as Executor
Defining the roles of mufti and qadi and conditions for overturning judgments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a qadi's judgment is revocable if it contradicts the preferred opinion (al-rajih) of the madhhab.

Core Idea
The mufti chooses rulings; the qadi executes them—his judgment stands only if it follows the rajih.

This lesson defines the distinct roles of the mufti and the qadi in Hanafi legal theory. While the mufti exercises ijtihad and selects opinions, the qadi is bound to implement the preferred ruling of the madhhab. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining when a judicial decision can be overturned.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the roles
Identify the definitions: mufti is a mujtahid with discretion; qadi is an executor bound to the Sharia. Note the quote from Tuhfat al-Hukkam: 'Executor of rulings by the Sharia.'
The mufti is 'mukhayyar' (chooser) and the qadi is 'munfidh' (executor).
2
Identify the conditions for overturning
Analyze the text's two scenarios: (a) if the weak opinion is outside the madhhab → void; (b) if inside the madhhab but the first qadi neglected the preferred opinion due to ignorance/negligence → revocable. If the first qadi correctly followed the rajih, the judgment stands.
The text states: 'if the first group was negligent... then the second group revokes its judgment.'
3
Consider the practical implication
The second qadi may revoke only if the first erred regarding the madhhab's preferred opinion. This ensures judgments adhere to the school's authoritative views.
This protects the integrity of the madhhab and prevents arbitrary overturning.
4
Apply to a hypothetical
Given a scenario where a qadi issues a judgment using a weak Hanafi opinion due to ignorance, determine whether a second qadi can overturn it. Apply the conditions from the text.
This cements the rule: overturning is allowed if the first qadi failed to follow the rajih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المفتى هو مخير فقط دون القاضي فانه منفذ للاحكام ولذلك وصفه في تحفة الحكام بقوله منفذ بالشرع للاحكام هذا هو الاصل فيه وقد يعترضه ما يعطل نفوذه ويعرقل اتمامه كا تحكم على الظلمة والجبابرة ولا يكون ذلك سها عنه باقي المسلمين المزعنين لو حكام رب العالمين وشريعة سيد المرسلين صلى الله عليه وسلم لوضعها بل يقبلوها ويقبلوا عليها سامعين مطيعين مذعنين ونصب جماعة المسلمين نقاض يفضل بهم الخصومات ويقطع المنازعات جائز بل يتعين في بعض الاحيان على الاعيان اذا وجد واسبيله اليه وعدم معارض فيه واجتماع الكلمة عليه والسوال الخامس المتفرع من الرابع جوابه فيه ومنه والسوال السادس فان كان الغلان خارج المذهب المتبع في هذه القضية فلا عبرة به ولا نظر اليه اذا لحكم الاول رافع للخلاف فيها وان كان داخل المذهب فكذلك مثى راعت الجماعة الاولى المراحج من النقول وجرت على الاصول واستوقت الشروط بالحصول اما اذا تساهلت الأولى في بناء الحكم ولم تجر على قواعد المذهب واصوله ولم تراع الراحج جهلا او تجاهلا او غفلة او عمد فالثانية نقضى حكمها بالطبع وذلك بنفسه يقال ويعمل به فى الحاكم الشرعي والله اعلم صلى الله على سيدنا محمد وعلى اله وسلم وكتبه بخط يده صالح بن الفضيل التونسى المدرس بالهجرة النبوى بالمدينة المنورة مقاتله مولاه ويحسن توفقه وغاية لولاه
The mufti is a mujtahid only, not the qadi, for he (the qadi) is an executor of rulings. Therefore, it is described in Tuhfat al-Hukkam: 'Executor of rulings by the Sharia.' This is the principle regarding him. However, there may oppose him that which nullifies his influence and impedes its completion, such as ruling over tyrants and oppressors, and that is not easy for the rest of the Muslims who submit to the rulers of the Lord of the worlds and the Sharia of the Master of the Messengers, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, but they accept it and listen to it obediently and submissively. And the appointment of judges by the general body of Muslims to decide disputes and settle conflicts is permissible, rather it becomes obligatory in some cases upon individuals when the means to it exist, no opposition to it, and consensus upon it. The fifth question derived from the fourth, its answer is in it and from it. The sixth question: If the weak opinion is outside the madhhab followed in this case, then no attention is paid to it and no consideration given to it because the first judgment removes the dispute in it. And if it is within the madhhab, then similarly, if the first group followed the preferred opinions from the transmitted texts and acted upon the principles and fulfilled the conditions of occurrence. But if the first group was negligent in establishing the judgment and did not follow the principles and foundations of the madhhab and did not observe the preferred opinion due to ignorance or willful ignorance, or negligence, or intentionally, then the second group revokes its judgment by nature, and that is said and acted upon in the Sharia court. And Allah knows best. May Allah bless our master Muhammad and his family and grant them peace. Written by his own hand: Salih ibn al-Fadil al-Tunisi, teacher in the Prophetic Hijrah in Medina Munawwara, may Allah protect him and grant him success. And the utmost is without Him.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the genre of rasm al-mufti, defining the roles of the mufti and the qadi and the conditions for overturning a judicial decision. The author states that the mufti is a mujtahid who has discretion in selecting rulings, whereas the qadi is merely an executor of the law. This is supported by a quotation from Tuhfat al-Hukkam. The qadi may face obstacles from tyrants, but the Muslims should accept the rulings submissively. The appointment of qadis by the general Muslim community is permissible and sometimes obligatory. The key legal issue addressed is whether a second qadi can overturn the judgment of a first qadi. The ruling distinguishes two scenarios: if the first judgment was based on an opinion outside the Hanafi madhhab, it is ineffective and the second qadi may ignore it. If it was within the madhhab but the first qadi followed a weak opinion due to ignorance or negligence, then the second qadi may revoke it. However, if the first qadi correctly applied the preferred opinion (al-rajih), the judgment stands and cannot be overturned. This reflects the Hanafi principle that the judgment of a qadi removes disagreement (raf' al-khilaf) only if it is based on sound madhhab principles.

Key Terms
مفتى (mufti): A qualified jurist who can exercise ijtihad and choose rulings, as opposed to the qadi who executes them.
قاضي (qadi): A judge appointed to implement the Sharia; he is an executor (munfidh) not a mujtahid.
الراجح (al-rajih): The preferred opinion within the Hanafi madhhab based on strongest evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when can a second qadi overturn the judgment of a first qadi?
A) Only if the first judgment was based on a weak opinion outside the Hanafi madhhab.
B) Only if the first judgment was based on a weak opinion within the Hanafi madhhab.
C) If the first qadi failed to follow the preferred opinion of the madhhab due to ignorance or negligence.
D) Never, because the first judgment removes all subsequent disputes.

Key Takeaway
A qadi's judgment stands only if it follows the preferred opinion of the madhhab; otherwise, it may be overturned by a subsequent qadi.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Conditions for a Valid Shari'ah Committee
Rasm al-mufti: conditions for Shari'ah committee

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid Shari'ah committee and apply the Hanafi principles to evaluate whether a given committee's decision is enforceable.

Core Idea
A Shari'ah committee must be unanimous, just, and learned; majority rule is invalid.

In the absence of a state-appointed qadi, the Hanafi school permits a committee of qualified Muslims to act as a substitute. However, this committee must meet strict conditions to ensure its decisions are Shari'ah-compliant. The following passage from Ashraf Ali Thanwi outlines the 'adalah, knowledge, and unanimity required, and warns against decisions by ignorant laymen.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three core conditions
The passage lists three main conditions: all members must be 'adil (just), at least one scholar must be involved (or the committee must consult scholars), and the decision must be unanimous.
2
Analyze the definition of 'adil
Note that 'adalah means avoiding major sins, not persisting in minor sins, and immediate repentance. This disqualifies sinners like usurers, beard-shavers, liars, and those who neglect prayers.
3
Trace the knowledge requirement
Ideally all members should be scholars; if not, at least one scholar must be included. If that is impossible, the committee must consult external scholars and follow their fatwa. Decisions based on laymen's own opinion are invalid even if correct.
4
Apply the unanimity rule
If members disagree, the case must be dismissed; majority opinion is not sufficient. However, the case can be resubmitted if circumstances change and unanimity is later achieved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دیندار اور اثر و رسوخ رکھنے والے مسلمانوں کی ایک جماعت بنائی جائے جو کہ کم از کم تین حضرات پر مشتمل ہو، اپنا معاملہ پیش کیا جائے اور وہ جماعت واقعہ کی تحقیق کر کے شریعت کے مطابق فیصلہ کر دے۔

شرعی کمیٹی کی شرائط: شرعی کمیٹی کو قاضی شرعی کے قائم مقام کرنے کے لیے چند شرائط ہیں جس کمیٹی میں یہ چند شرائط موجود نہ ہوں وہ شرعاً معتبر نہ ہوگی۔

عادل کی تعریف: کم از کم تین آدمیوں کی جماعت ہو، ایک یا دو آدمی فیصلہ کریں تو وہ معتبر نہیں۔ اس کمیٹی کے تمام ارکان کا عادل ہونا شرط ہے، اور عادل وہ شخص ہے جو تمام کبیرہ گناہوں سے بچتا ہو اور صغائر پر مصر نہ ہو اور اگر کوئی گناہ سرزد ہو جاتا ہے تو فوراً توبہ کر لیتا ہو۔ لہذا سود خور، رشوت لینے والا، داڑھی منڈانے والا، جھوٹ بولنے والا اور بے نماز اس جماعت کا رکن نہیں بن سکتا۔ اگر بدقسمتی سے کسی جگہ کے بااثر لوگ دیندار نہ ہوں تو یہ تدبیر کر لی جائے کہ وہ بااثر اشخاص چند دینداروں کو اختیار دے دیں تاکہ شرکت فیصلہ کی نسبت دیندار جماعت کی طرف ہو اور ان بااثر اشخاص کو کوشش کا ثواب حاصل ہو جائے۔

فیصلہ میں علماء کی شرکت لازم اور شرط ہے، کیونکہ فیصلہ میں عام آدمی کی شرکت لازم ہے، صرف عوام کی جماعت کو اختیار دے کر قاضی کے قائم مقام نہیں ہو سکتا، اس لیے یہ چاہیے کہ جماعت کے سب ارکان اہل علم ہوں۔ ور اگر یہی نہ ہو تو کم از کم ایک عالم کو ضرور جماعت کا رکن بنائیں اور دور سے اس معاملہ کے تمام پہلوؤں کو اس عالم صاحب سے خوب سمجھ کر رائے قائم کریں۔ اور اگر کسی کی یہ بھی ممکن نہ ہو تو پھر یہ لازم ہے کہ جماعت کے ارکان معاملہ کی روداد مرتب کر کے علماء محققین سے ہر ہر جزئی کا حکم دریافت کریں اور جو ان کا فتوی ہو اس کے موافق فیصلہ کیا جائے، اگر ایسا نہ کیا بلکہ عوام نے محض اپنی رائے سے فیصلہ کر دیا تو وہ حکم نافذ نہ ہوگا اور فیصلہ بالکل بے کار اور غیر معتبر ہوگا، اگر چہ وہ فیصلہ شریعت کے موافق بھی ہو۔

اختلافی فیصلہ کی حیثیت: چوتھی شرط یہ ہے کہ شرعی کمیٹی کے تمام ارکان متفقہ فیصلہ دیں، اگر رائے مختلف رہے اور کثرت رائے کی بنا پر فیصلہ کرنا چاہیں تو وہ فیصلہ معتبر نہ ہوگا، پس اگر ارکان میں اختلاف رہے تو مقدمہ خارج کر دیا جائے۔

اگر اختلاف رائے کی وجہ سے فیصلہ نہ ہو سکے: اگر اختلاف رائے کی وجہ سے فیصلہ نہ ہو سکا تھا تو وہ درخواست ہمیشہ کے لیے مسترد نہ ہو جائے گی بلکہ مستفیقہ کو اختیار ہوگا کہ معاملہ کی حالت بدل جائے یا ضرورت کی شدت بڑھ جائے تو دوبارہ درخواست پیش کرے اور دوبارہ درخواست دینے پر اگر ارکان کی رائے متفق ہو جائے تو تفریق کی جائے۔

اب ان اسباب کو بیان کیا جاتا ہے جن کی وجہ سے عورت کو نکاح فسخ کرانے کا اختیار حاصل ہوتا ہے اور اختیار ہونے کی جو شرطیں ہیں وہ سب لکھی جاتی ہیں، آنکل لوگ فسخ نکاح کا اختیار ہونے میں شرائط کی رعایت نہیں کرتے اور بغیر وجہ شرعی نکاح فسخ کر ڈالتے ہیں لیکن اس قسم کے فسخ کا شرعاً اعتبار نہیں ہے اور دوسرے شخص سے ایسی عورت کا نکاح باطل ہے اسی وجہ سے ان کا خصوصی خیال رکھنا لازم ہے۔
A group of religious and influential Muslims should be formed, comprising at least three persons, present their case, and that group should investigate the event and decide according to Shari'ah.

Conditions of the Shari'ah Committee: There are several conditions for making the Shari'ah Committee a substitute for the Shari'ah judge; any committee lacking these conditions will not be considered valid according to Shari'ah.

Definition of Adil (Just): It must be a group of at least three men; if one or two men decide, it is not valid. All members of this committee must be just ('adil). A just person is one who avoids all major sins and does not persist in minor sins, and if he commits a sin, he repents immediately. Therefore, a usurer, bribe-taker, beard-shaver, liar, and one who does not pray cannot become a member of this group. If unfortunately, the influential people in a place are not religious, then this arrangement should be made: those influential persons should delegate authority to some religious persons so that the participation in the decision is attributed to the religious group, and the influential persons may obtain the reward of effort.

Participation of scholars in the decision is necessary and a condition, because in a decision, participation of laymen is necessary; a group of laymen cannot be made a substitute for the qadi merely by giving them authority. Therefore, it is required that all members of the group be scholars. If this is not possible, then at least one scholar must be made a member of the group, and from a distance, all aspects of the case should be thoroughly understood from that scholar and then an opinion formed. And if that too is not possible for someone, then it is necessary that the members of the group compile a report of the case and seek the ruling on each and every detail from verifying scholars, and decide according to their fatwa. If this is not done, but rather laymen decide merely on their own opinion, then that judgment will not be enforced, and the decision will be completely useless and unreliable, even if that decision is in accordance with Shari'ah.

Status of a Disputed Decision: The fourth condition is that all members of the Shari'ah Committee give a unanimous decision. If opinions differ and they wish to decide based on majority opinion, that decision will not be valid. Therefore, if there is disagreement among the members, the case should be dismissed.

If a decision cannot be reached due to difference of opinion: If a decision could not be reached due to difference of opinion, that application will not be permanently rejected; rather, the petitioner will have the option that if the state of the case changes or the urgency increases, they may submit the application again, and upon resubmission, if the members' opinions become unanimous, then annulment may be granted.

Now the causes are being explained due to which a woman obtains the right to annul her marriage, and all the conditions for having that right are written. Many people do not observe the conditions for having the right to annul a marriage and annul the marriage without a Shari'ah reason, but such an annulment is not valid according to Shari'ah, and the marriage of such a woman with another person is invalid; therefore, it is necessary to pay special attention to this.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents the rasm (methodological rules) for establishing a Shari'ah committee that can act as a substitute for a qadi in rendering judgments. The author, Ashraf Ali Thanwi, outlines the conditions under which such a committee is valid according to the Hanafi madhhab.

The passage begins by stating the basic requirement: a committee of at least three religious and influential Muslims should be formed to investigate a case and decide according to Shari'ah. This committee acts as a representative of the qadi.

The first condition is that all members must be 'adil (just). The author defines 'adil as one who avoids major sins (kaba'ir) and does not persist in minor sins (sagha'ir), and repents immediately upon sinning. Thus, sinners such as usurers, bribe-takers, those who shave their beards (a sign of disobedience), liars, and those who neglect prayers cannot be members. If the local influential people are not righteous, they should delegate authority to upright individuals so the decision is attributed to the righteous.

The second condition is that the committee must include scholars. Ideally, all members should be scholars; if not, at least one scholar must be included. If that is impossible, the committee must consult external scholars and follow their fatwa. If laymen decide based on their own opinion, the judgment is invalid even if it happens to be correct.

The third condition is that the committee's decision must be unanimous. If there is disagreement, the case is dismissed. Majority opinion is not sufficient.

The fourth condition is that if a decision cannot be reached, the case is not permanently rejected; it can be resubmitted if circumstances change or need intensifies, and if unanimity is then achieved, the annulment may be granted.

The passage then transitions to the topic of khul' or annulment of marriage (faskh al-nikah), noting that many people do not fulfill the conditions and annul without justification, which is invalid. This final part is introductory and the detailed causes are not given in this passage.

The authority weight is that of a later fatwa collection (mutakhkhirin), as Thanwi is a later Hanafi scholar.

Key Terms
عادل (adil): A person who avoids major sins, does not persist in minor sins, and repents immediately if he sins.
شرعی کمیٹی (shar'i committee): A group of at least three just, learned Muslims who act as a substitute for a qadi in issuing judgments.
فسخ نکاح (faskh al-nikah): Annulment of marriage based on Shari'ah grounds, such as impotence, apostasy, or severe harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the minimum number of members required for a valid Shari'ah committee according to this passage?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Five

Key Takeaway
A Shari'ah committee must be unanimous, just, and learned; otherwise its decisions are invalid.

Verifying Husband's Impotence: The Procedure of Examination (Mu'aayana)
Ruling on procedure for verifying husband's impotence via examination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct procedure for verifying a wife's claim of husband's impotence and apply the rule that the qadi appoints a trustworthy examiner.

Core Idea
A wife's claim of husband's impotence is resolved by a qadi-appointed examination, not direct confrontation.

This passage addresses a common marital dispute: the wife claims her husband is incapable of intercourse, and the husband denies. How does a qadi resolve this? The Hanafi school outlines a clear procedure: not by forcing the wife to prove it, but by appointing a neutral third party to examine the husband. This ensures both testimony and modesty are preserved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and denial
The wife claims the husband is impotent (majbub) and the husband denies. The qadi cannot decide without evidence.
Majbub means the male organ is cut off, rendering intercourse impossible.
2
Determine the need for examination
If no decision can be made without examination, then examination becomes permissible and necessary.
Examination is a means to verify the claim when testimony alone is insufficient.
3
Show the role of the qadi
The qadi does not personally examine the husband. Instead, he appoints a trustworthy person (mu'tabar shakhs) for the task.
The qadi remains the authority but delegates the act of examination.
4
Show the appointment and reporting
The qadi instructs the appointee: 'Examine and inform us whether the woman is truthful or the man is truthful.'
The examiner reports back to the qadi, who then makes the ruling.
5
Note the source limitations
This passage is a brief summary from the Hidayah; full details are in other fiqh works. A mufti should consult those when needed.
This is a practical rule for muftis, not an exhaustive treatment.

Ruling on Dissolution Due to Husband's Insanity
Summary of rulings for mufti on dissolution due to husband's insanity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on dissolution of marriage due to husband's insanity, distinguishing pre- and post-nuptial cases, and identify the mufta bihi position when cross-madhhab considerations arise.

Core Idea
Post-nuptial insanity gives no automatic faskh in Hanafi; only for severe necessity and treated as talāq.

This passage is a practical rasm al-mufti summary. It outlines when a wife can seek annulment due to husband's insanity, distinguishing two scenarios: insanity at time of marriage (pre-nuptial) and insanity after marriage (post-nuptial). The Hanafi school's default position is that post-nuptial insanity does not grant the wife an automatic option (khiyar); only the Maliki school grants it. The mufti must approach such cases cautiously, ensuring severe necessity, and when dissolving, treat it as a divorce (talāq) rather than a pure annulment (faskh) to maintain caution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of insanity
Determine whether the husband's insanity existed at the time of the marriage contract (pre-nuptial) or occurred after the marriage (post-nuptial). This classification determines the legal ruling.
The passage explicitly distinguishes these two forms.
2
Apply the Hanafi rule
For pre-nuptial insanity, Imam Muhammad holds that the wife has the option of faskh. For post-nuptial insanity, the Hanafi school does not grant an automatic option; only the Maliki school does.
In Hanafi, post-nuptial insanity is not considered a valid ground for automatic faskh.
3
Assess necessity and procedure
If post-nuptial, only dissolve in case of severe necessity (darurah). The procedure involves the qadi granting a one-year waiting period for treatment, then upon request, the wife may be given the option to dissolve.
The qadi must verify the insanity and give a one-year respite.
4
Determine the legal effect
If dissolution is for pre-nuptial insanity, it is faskh (annulment), not talāq. If for post-nuptial, it is considered talāq out of caution, and the mufti should consult Maliki scholars for verification.
This affects the wife's entitlement to mahr and iddah.
5
Apply the conditions for the option
The wife must not have consented to the marriage knowing the insanity (pre-nuptial) and must not have consented to remain after learning of post-nuptial insanity. Once consent is given, the option is void.
Consent includes any explicit or implicit acceptance.

Source Text
