Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
advanced
10 min
Accepting Judgeship from a Non-Muslim Ruler: The Hanafi Legal Argument
Narrative of scholarly debate resolving the apparent conflict between the Qur'anic verse and the need to accept judicial appointments from non-Muslim rulers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between tawliya (conferment of authority) and shart (condition) to determine the validity of accepting a judicial appointment from a non-Muslim ruler.

Core Idea
The non-Muslim ruler's appointment is only a condition, not a conferment of authority — the real authority comes from the Muslim community.

This passage addresses a critical issue for Muslims living under non-Muslim governments: can a Muslim accept a judgeship from a non-Muslim ruler? The Hanafi scholars debate this, using the Qur'anic verse that seemingly forbids non-Muslims having authority over believers. Through careful reasoning, they distinguish between the ruler's role as a mere facilitator and the true source of authority from the Muslims themselves. Understanding this argument is essential for muftis dealing with contemporary issues of Muslim minorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core objection
The objection: accepting judgeship from a non-Muslim ruler violates the Qur'anic principle (4:141) that disbelievers should have no authority over believers.
2
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: the appointment is not a conferment of authority (tawliya) but a condition (shart) that removes the obstacle of enforcement; the real entrustment is from the Muslim community.
3
Analyze the analogy with a usurping ruler
The analogy: just as a judge can accept appointment from a usurper (baghi mutaghallib) because the usurper provides the power of enforcement, similarly, a non-Muslim ruler's appointment is permissible.
4
Recognize the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The consequence: accepting this position allows Muslim judges to function under non-Muslim governments, ensuring the continuation of Islamic legal processes.

عبد الكريم عفی عنہ
At that time, whatever matter seemed extremely important, drawing attention to it seemed necessary. If by your blessed pen the statement of the Hanafi position is also included as an appendix in this treatise, it would be better. The need and importance of this issue... The remaining margin of the previous page says that this is contrary to the Qur'anic text 'Allah will never make for the disbelievers a way over the believers'... The answer to this was written here: 'Taqlid al-Kafir' is not based on the authority of the sultanate, but the reality is that the duty of appointment (of judges) that devolves upon the Muslims, they cannot perform it because the power of enforcement is in [the ruler's] hands. But when the non-Muslim government has appointed someone as qadi or governor, the obstacle to power is removed. Therefore its interpretation will be that the sultan or the non-Muslim ruler has appointed someone to the office of qadi; in reality, that entrustment is from the side of the Muslims, upon which even the silence of the Muslims can be valid. And the non-Muslim government is only a messenger, and this permission of messengership is a condition for obtaining the power of enforcement. The conclusion is that the appointment of a qadi from the side of the non-Muslim government is not the conferment (tawliya) of qadiship but a condition for it. So by this appointment the authority of the non-Muslims over the Muslims is completely removed. So ponder and be grateful. Also, in the answer here it was written that its precedent is the acceptance of judgeship from a usurping ruler, and Shams al-A'imma gave the same direction which is mentioned in the margin of Takhrij al-Rifaq (؟)...
On this conclusion, from his words 'And the meaning in it' to his words 'And what has been obtained'... After that, Maulana Sajjad Sahib, probably in Jumada al-Akhira, came to Thana Bhawan. Maulana Kifayatullah Sahib and others were also with him. At that time Maulana Sajjad Sahib also made great efforts to declare the appointment of a judge from the common people as correct, and he prepared the aforementioned objection to 'Taqlid al-Kafir' with great care. Hakeem al-Ummah said to this humble one that perhaps some answer was written from here. This humble one searched in Ta'liq Imdad al-Ahkam, Volume 2, and presented that answer, which contained both issues, i.e., the invalidity of appointing a judge from the common people... besides, the practical times of the panchayat are very many, and it will be very difficult to observe these conditions. Wa al-salam only.
Abu al-Muhasin Muhammad Sajjad, may Allah...[date] 25th Rabi' al-Awwal 1353.
(Remaining margin of previous page) And there is sufficient discussion on the validity of accepting judgeship from a non-Muslim. Upon hearing this, Maulana Husayn Ahmad Sahib said that now there is no objection left in this matter. Maulana Kifayatullah Sahib first questioned with surprise at this statement. Then after brief conversation he himself conceded. After that, this humble one became tired. In those days, this humble one was ill, so he asked permission from the most holy presence. He said that sufficient time has passed and necessary discussion has also taken place. Now it seems appropriate that whatever objections there are, they should be written down; they will be dealt with calmly. With this, the respected scholars wrote several questions, some of which had already been answered, and some were sent to Medina, considering it appropriate. When the answer came from there, it was published in the form of a supplement. See Fatawa Manakiyya, at the end, under the title 'al-Istifta' bi al-marra al-khamisah' 12.

Present: 'Abd al-Karim, may Allah pardon him. The mention of these difficult occasions was in those questions which have been mentioned in the previous margin. When the answer came from Medina, everything was completed, so it was considered sufficient to publish that answer. Wa Allah al-Muwaffiq.
'Abd al-Karim, may Allah pardon him.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative recounting a scholarly debate among Hanafi jurists regarding two interconnected issues: (1) the validity of appointing a judge from among the common people (nash al-qadi min al-'ammah), and (2) the permissibility of accepting a judicial appointment from a non-Muslim ruler (taqlid al-qada min al-kafir). The text records the positions of Maulana Sajjad, Maulana Kifayatullah, Maulana Husayn Ahmad, and others, and references earlier authorities such as Shams al-A'imma. The central legal reasoning revolves around the Qur'anic verse 'Allah will never make for the disbelievers a way over the believers' (Surah al-Nisa, 4:141). The Hanafi scholars resolve the apparent conflict by arguing that when a non-Muslim ruler appoints a qadi, he does not exercise true authority; rather, he merely facilitates the implementation of Islamic judicial functions. The actual appointment is considered to be from the Muslim community, as they are the ones who have the duty to establish a judiciary but lack the coercive power. The non-Muslim ruler's appointment removes the obstacle (mani') of enforcement, allowing Muslims to fulfill their obligation. Therefore, the appointee becomes a legitimate qadi from the perspective of Islamic law. The passage also mentions the use of panchayats (informal councils) as a practical alternative but notes their many conditions. The discussion concludes with the scholars reaching consensus (or at least Maulana Kifayatullah conceding) on the validity under the given circumstances. The later part of the text describes the process of documenting and publishing the final answers, indicating a careful methodology for issuing fatwas on contemporary issues.

Key Terms
نصب القاضي من العامة (nash al-qadi min al-'ammah): Appointment of a judge from among the common (non-specialist) people; the text discusses its validity.
تقلد القضا من الكافر (taqlid al-qada min al-kafir): Accepting the office of judgeship from a non-Muslim ruler; the text argues it is permissible as a means to fulfill the Islamic duty of establishing justice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, why is the non-Muslim ruler's appointment of a qadi not considered 'giving the disbelievers a way over believers'?
A) Because the verse is abrogated.
B) Because the ruler is not a real kafir.
C) Because the appointment is only a condition for enforcement, not a conferment of authority.
D) Because the ruler converts at the time of appointment.

Key Takeaway
Accepting a judgeship from a non-Muslim ruler is permissible because the ruler's appointment is a condition (shart), not a conferment of authority (tawliya), thereby preserving Islamic legal processes.
